        gptPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.page ? digitalData.page.page : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/adfm.202402178')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="e006c78b6abf4ac3bc5af31608215e77"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="e006c78b6abf4ac3bc5af31608215e77">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '75986c38-1cb0-44da-b1d0-412cfe1228e6'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        (function() {
            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {
                var store_domain = 'https://advanced.onlinelibrary.wiley.com';
                var session_id = 'FB5283BC094EF04C675D847CB2D41818';
                var url = ('https:' == document.location.protocol ? 'https://' : 'http://')
                    + "beacon.riskified.com?shop=" + store_domain + "&sid=" + session_id;
                var s = document.createElement('script');
                s.type = 'text/javascript';
                s.async = true;
                s.src = url;
                var x = document.getElementsByTagName('script')[0];
                x.parentNode.insertBefore(s, x);
            }
            if (window.attachEvent)
                window.attachEvent('onload', riskifiedBeaconLoad)
            else
                window.addEventListener('load', riskifiedBeaconLoad, false);
        })();

                     <p>E-mail: <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:ifeyliu@fjnu.edu.cn"><span>ifeyliu@fjnu.edu.cn</span></a>; <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:ifeyzhao@fjnu.edu.cn"><span>ifeyzhao@fjnu.edu.cn</span></a>; <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:vc@nwpu.edu.cn"><span>vc@nwpu.edu.cn</span></a></p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Liu/Yang">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Guo/Yunjie" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a3" data-db-target-for="a3" aria-controls="a3" aria-haspopup="true" id="a3_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Yunjie Guo</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a3" aria-labelledby="a3_Ctrl" role="region" id="a3">

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d216912183" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are promising alternatives to lithium-ion batteries with similar working principles, cell structures, and material systems. However, attaining long-term stability and high-capacity performance of SIBs at ultrahigh rates remains a significant challenge due to the large ionic radius and slow kinetic behavior of Na<sup>+</sup>. Herein, a novel robust anode with a dual-biomimetic gradient hierarchical architecture is proposed and provide a simple strategy to fabricate this sulfur-doped mesoporous carbon concave hollow sphere/Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>x</sub> MXene (SCMX) anode. In addition, Cu<sub>2</sub>S nanoparticles are in situ embedded into the SCMX architecture by electrochemical induction during the cycling process to act as active sites, which enhances the rate performance and cycling stability. Relying on its hybrid architecture and in situ formed Cu<sub>2</sub>S, this SCMX anode can achieve high ion accessibility, rich active sites, rapid charge transfer, and favorable structure stability, as disclosed by in/ex situ characterizations. As a result, it exhibits high reversible capacity (745.6&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), ultrahigh-rate capability (380.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 50.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), and long cycling stability (98.2% capacity retention after 10&nbsp;000 cycles at 80.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). This work is anticipated to accelerate the development of high-performance SIBs and offer distinctive inspiration for the design of electrode structures/systems.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202402178-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202402178-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) are widely used in portable electronic devices and electric vehicles due to their lightweight, high energy density, and low self-discharge rate.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402178-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402178-bib-0001_R_d216912174e456" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nature Nat. Rev. Chem.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, limited lithium reserves and its rapid depletion make it difficult to meet the growing demand for energy storage in the future.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402178-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402178-bib-0002_R_d216912174e461" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Nat. Energy">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Sodium-based energy storage systems, due to the abundance, affordability, and easy accessibility of sodium resources, emerge as promising and rising alternatives.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402178-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402178-bib-0003_R_d216912174e466" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nat. Mater. Nat. Rev. Mater. Nat. Rev. Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The advent of the Internet of Things (IoT) and intelligent cities—that interconnect the sensors, actuators, communication devices, data analytics engines, and the like—stimulate the growing need for a large number of compact and efficient energy storage devices for high power delivery.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402178-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402178-bib-0004_R_d216912174e472" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nat. Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the larger radius of the sodium ions (1.02&nbsp;Å) hinders their insertion in the anode (especially at high rates), leading to sluggish kinetics, large volume changes, and poor power performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402178-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402178-bib-0005_R_d216912174e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Sci. Adv.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, constructing a stable structure with fast sodium storage behavior is crucial for the development of high-rate sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).</p>
         
         <p>The most commercially promising candidate for anode materials is hard carbon (HC), owing to its adjustable physicochemical properties and its cheap and easy availability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402178-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402178-bib-0006_R_d216912174e485" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, the electrochemical performance of HC, including its rate capability, cycling stability, and initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE), falls significantly short when compared to those of graphite anode in LIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402178-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402178-bib-0007_R_d216912174e490" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To improve the fast sodium storage capability, optimizing the structure properties of HC has been widely explored as an effective way, such as increasing the interlayer spacing to accelerate the intercalation/deintercalation rate of sodium ions in the graphitic domains,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402178-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402178-bib-0008_R_d216912174e495" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Energy Environ. Sci.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> doping heteroatoms or introducing defects to provide more adsorption sites,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402178-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402178-bib-0009_R_d216912174e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> constructing suitable morphologies to tune the accessible active sites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402178-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402178-bib-0010_R_d216912174e506" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, these strategies of relying solely on HC remain unsatisfactory in achieving high-rate capability (&gt;20&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), efficient electron conduction, and stable spatial structure.</p>
         
         <p>Constructing spatial heterojunction architectures has emerged as a promising strategy by using an integrated win-win heterogeneous material/structure system, three heterojunction ways of HC are shown as follows: 1) HC as a coating layer that limits the volume expansion of the active component upon sodium ion insertion (such as MoS<sub>1.5</sub>Te<sub>0.5</sub>@C nanocables,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402178-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402178-bib-0011_R_d216912174e520" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Nat. Commun.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> multi-yolk–shell Fe<sub>1−x</sub>S@C,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402178-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402178-bib-0012_R_d216912174e528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and single-atom Co-doped MoS<sub>2</sub>/C composites,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402178-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402178-bib-0013_R_d216912174e535" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> etc.); 2) HC as a backbone for active components (such as VN nanodots/carbon nanofibers 0D/1D heterostructure,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402178-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402178-bib-0014_R_d216912174e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - ACS Nano">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> CoO/Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles (NPs)@carbon hollow polyhedra 0D/3D heterostructure,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402178-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402178-bib-0015_R_d216912174e550" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> MoTe<sub>2</sub>@porous carbon networks 2D/3D heterostructure,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402178-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402178-bib-0016_R_d216912174e558" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Energy Stor. Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> etc.); 3) composite HC with low-dimensional conductive materials to form conductive networks (such as 1D carbon nanotubes,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402178-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402178-bib-0017_R_d216912174e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Mater. Chem. A">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> 2D graphene and MXene<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402178-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402178-bib-0018_R_d216912174e569" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Chem. Eng. J. ACS Nano">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>). Although the above strategies help to improve rate performance to some extent, there are still some problems: 1) carbon coating strategy only mitigates volume expansion at lower current densities and performs poorly at high rates and long cycles; 2) HC matrix has a low degree of graphitization and poor electrical conductivity, making it difficult to transport electrons efficiently; 3) compounding with conductive materials does not effectively increase the number of active sites, resulting in alower capacity. It is still a challenge to prepare HC-based materials with abundant active sites, stable spatial structure, and continuous conductive network by a simple synthesis method to realize high rate and long cycle SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>Nature evolves elaborated structures and functionality in multidimensions during long-term natural selection and environmental adaptation, offering kaleidoscopic bioinspiration for designing principles and approaches.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402178-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402178-bib-0019_R_d216912174e577" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Science">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Coral polyps prefer to live in oceanic environments with fast water currents (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402178-fig-0001">1a</a> left), which efficiently capture abundant plankton in the fast water currents by their bowl-shaped structure with tentacles and surface mucus layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402178-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402178-bib-0020_R_d216912174e588" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Annu. Rev. Microbiol.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nutrients acquired by individual coral polyps can be efficiently transported to the whole colony by the laminar cavity of coenosarc (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402178-fig-0001">1a</a> middle). It is worth noting that coral polyps can form symbiosis with parasitic zooxanthellae, with the former providing protection to the latter and the latter providing nutrients and oxygen to the former (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402178-fig-0001">1a</a> right). The biological behavior of coral polyps in fast water currents is similar to ion adsorption, conversion, and transportation of SIBs at high rates (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402178-fig-0001">1b</a>). In terms of the challenge of the structure stability of anodes, mammalian spine—which comprises continuous flexible ligaments and separated rigid vertebras to enable trunk support and flexibility under various body motion states—may offer some inspiration in designing spatial architecture with enhancing mechanical characteristics (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402178-fig-0001">1c</a>).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202402178-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/241780f8-4d1e-458e-8867-bee5aebf9d32/adfm202402178-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/241780f8-4d1e-458e-8867-bee5aebf9d32/adfm202402178-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/556771e2-d47e-43b3-a89a-a040907c7750/adfm202402178-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/241780f8-4d1e-458e-8867-bee5aebf9d32/adfm202402178-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202402178-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202402178" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Bioinspired design and comparison performance of SCMX6. a) Schematic of coral polyps. Coral polyps colony in the sea (left), enlarged cross-section of two coral polyps connected by the laminar cavity of a coenosarc (middle), and enlarged parts of the coral polyp (right). b) Schematic of the SCMX6. Electrode in SIBs (left), cross-section schematic with two carbon bowls connected by laminar MXene nanosheets (middle), and enlarged atomic-level structure (right). c) Schematic of the preparation process and bionic spine function of SCMX6 that was obtained by electrostatic co-assembly of MCCHS with positive charges and MXene with negative charges. Schematic and SEM image; scale bar: 300&nbsp;nm. Diagram of the spine and spine in bend and hypokinesis. d) Cycling performance and capacity of SCMX6 versus other reported anode materials in SIBs, such as the reported advanced carbon-based and transition metal-oxide/sulfide/selenide/phosphide-based materials (Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Herein, we propose a dual-biomimetic (both coral polyp-like and spine-like) gradient hierarchical architecture, boosting the ion accessibility for ultrahigh-rate and durable sodium-ion energy storage. As a proof of concept, we integrate mesoporous carbon concave hollow spheres (MCCHS) with few-layered 2D-conductive-network MXene (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402178-fig-0001">1b</a>), after sulfuration termed as SCMX. During operation, Cu<sub>2</sub>S NPs, as active sites to enhance sodium storage performance, are in-situ formed by electrochemically induced reaction of S species in carbon and copper current collectors. The C−S bonds and Cu<sub>2</sub>S NPs, acting as tentacles of coral polyps and zooxanthellae, can quickly capture sodium ions; in addition, the 3D concave hollow sphere structure can independently withstand the intensive volume changes. Moreover, the monolayer MCCHS were adhered and co-assembled onto 2D MXene surface by electrostatic interactions to form a flex-rigid spine-like structure, which further constructed a more complex gradient hierarchical architecture by vacuum filtration as well as heat treatment for chemical welding and sulfuration (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402178-fig-0001">1c</a>).</p>
         
         <p>This dual-biomimetic gradient hierarchical architecture simultaneously endows ultrahigh-rate anode with the following comprehensive advantages: 1) accelerated ion storage and following ion transport/transfer processes originating from MCCHS ultrathin mesoporous surface, highly accessible active sites (including in situ-formed Cu<sub>2</sub>S NPs), and joint heterostructure interface; 2) rapid electron transportation that is facilitated by continuous spatial conductive networks based on 2D MXene (standing for coenosarc); 3) long-term cycle stability and durability that is attributed to hierarchical flex-rigid hybrid structure, carbon concave hollow sphere, and mesoporous surface to accommodate volume expansion/shrinkage from macro to nano scale. Relying on this unique dual-biomimetic architecture, the SCMX anode exhibited a high capacity of 745.6&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 150 cycles at 0.2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, a good rate capability of 380.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 50&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, and long cycling stability up to 10&nbsp;000 cycles (98.2% capacity retention) at 80&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. For full-cell type sodium-ion hybrid capacitors (SICs), the SICs can still maintain an energy density of up to 50.68&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> when subjected to a high power density of 35&nbsp;878&nbsp;W&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202402178-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202402178-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202402178-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202402178-sec-0030-title">2.1 Design and Fabrication of Dual-Biomimetic Gradient Hierarchical Architecture</h3>
            
            <p>Drawing inspiration from the structure and functionality of coral polyps living in the sea with fast water currents (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402178-fig-0001">1a</a>), we have meticulously optimized the design of electrodes for high-rate energy storage. Bowl-shaped coral polyps with tentacles and surface mucus layers spread evenly on the surface of reef skeleton and are tightly connected by laminar coenosarc structure, which evolve the following biological behavior: 1) efficiently capturing abundant planktonic supply in the fast water by their bowl-shaped structure with tentacles and surface mucus layer, 2) rapidly transporting the digested nutrients to the whole colony by the laminar cavity of coenosarc (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402178-fig-0001">1a</a> middle and right). Such biological behavior in fast water currents is similar to ions adsorbing, converting, and transporting properties in batteries working at high rates (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402178-fig-0001">1b</a>), inspiring us to design gradient hierarchical architecture for high-rate SIBs. We co-assembled monolayer bowl-like MCCHS on the surface of laminar Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>x</sub> MXene, similar to connected coral polyps by laminar coenosarc. Rich C−S bonds present on the surface of the bowl-like carbon mimic the surface mucous layer, facilitating the swift capture of sodium ions in the electrolyte. The heterogeneous interfaces of MCCHS and MXene were tightly connected and jointed by a chemical connection (Ti−O−C bonds) through annealing similar to the bio-connection. Moreover, the few-layer MXene nanosheets provide fast electron transport channels, analogous to the laminar coenosarc structure facilitating nutrient delivery. In-situ formed Cu<sub>2</sub>S NPs during the cycling are embedded in carbon, like post-harboring zooxanthellae in coral polyps.</p>
            
            <p>The structural design is similar to a bionic spine in section view. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402178-fig-0001">1c</a> depicts the co-assembly process of MCCHS and MXene, where positively charged MCCHS is adsorbed onto the negatively charged MXene surface by electrostatic force to obtain spine-like assemblies. Spines (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402178-fig-0001">1c</a> left), made up of flexible ligaments and rigid vertebras, exhibit excellent structure flexibility and durability when suffering mechanic deformation; for example, it allows the body to switch between a wide variety of postures. Therefore, we combined flexible 2D laminar MXene and close-packed monolayer rigid MCCHS to form a spine-like architecture to mitigate volume expansion/shrinkage during cycling (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402178-fig-0001">1c</a> middle). Thanks to the novel dual-biomimetic design of SCMX6 as an anode material, high-capacity and long-cycle SIBs at ultrahigh current densities were demonstrated, compared with advanced anodes that have been reported so far (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402178-fig-0001">1d</a>).</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202402178-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202402178-sec-0040-title">2.2 Morphological and Physical Characterization</h3>
            
            <p>The dual-biomimetic hierarchical architectures were achieved by tailoring the co-assembled process of MXene and MCCHS, which were demonstrated by scanning electron microscope (SEM) characterization in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402178-fig-0002">2a–d</a>. Schematics for the preparation of MCCHS and single or few-layer MXene are shown in Figures&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information), respectively. MCCHS exhibit homogeneous concave hollow spheres morphology with a shell thickness of 16&nbsp;nm (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). MXene exhibit thin nanosheets morphology with a thickness of ≈2.8&nbsp;nm (about two MXene layers, Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). In the absence of MXene, pure MCCHS tend to stack randomly and fail to establish a continuous conductive network (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402178-fig-0002">2a</a>). Sulfur-doped MCCHS (SC) shows uniform concave hollow sphere morphology with diameters of 250–300&nbsp;nm (size of 180&nbsp;nm in concave direction). By introducing a small amount of negatively charged MXene (3&nbsp;wt.% relative to MCCHS), electrostatic forces facilitated the attraction of superfluous MCCHS to the surface of MXene, resulting in a multilayered MCCHS stacking and assembly on MXene (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402178-fig-0002">2b</a>). At this stage, the thickness of SCMX3 is ≈1&nbsp;µm or larger, corresponding to more than 4 layers of MCCHS (the thickness of few-layer MXene can be disregarded). The densely stacked multilayer MCCHS of SCMX3 leads to a limited conductive network that impedes sodium ion diffusion and electron transfer. Upon increasing the amount of MXene (6&nbsp;wt.% relative to MCCHS), homogeneous monolayer (or quasi-monolayer) MCCHSs co-assembly onto the surface of MXene were obtained due to the controllable electrostatic attraction (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402178-fig-0002">2c</a>). The thickness of the SCMX6 assemblies ranged from 420 to 550&nbsp;nm, which corresponds to the thickness of quasi-monolayer MCCHS on both sides of the MXene surface (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402178-fig-0002">2c</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). The SCMX6 possessed both a coral polyp-like and a spine-like structure/function at the nano-/microscale. The unique dual-biomimetic structure of SCMX6 facilitates rapid sodium ion transfer channels, a large number of highly accessible active sites, and manageable volume expansion, implying exceptional electrochemical performance. When mixing superfluous MXene (such as 12&nbsp;wt.% relative to MCCHS) with MCCHS, MCCHS could not completely cover the surface of MXene uniformly, thereby leading to MXene stacking and thus impeding the migration of sodium ions (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402178-fig-0002">2d</a>).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202402178-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4dcaea58-2d2a-4014-96d8-c44750e4cce7/adfm202402178-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/4dcaea58-2d2a-4014-96d8-c44750e4cce7/adfm202402178-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/315aebd4-7ac1-4161-a2ab-23f822f36ecf/adfm202402178-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4dcaea58-2d2a-4014-96d8-c44750e4cce7/adfm202402178-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202402178-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202402178" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Morphological and physical characterization. a–d) SEM images and the corresponding schematic of SC a), SCMX3 b), SCMX6 c), and SCMX12 d). Scale bars: 1&nbsp;µm. e) Zeta potentials of positive MCCHS and negative MXene. f) XRD patterns. g) Raman spectroscopy spectra. h) High-resolution XPS Ti 2<i>p</i> spectra of SCMX6 and MXene film. i) High-resolution XPS S 2<i>p</i> spectra of SC and SCMX6. j) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption-desorption isotherms.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The electrostatic force attraction that induces the co-assembled SCMX was confirmed by the zeta potentials of −27.91 and 26.57&nbsp;mV for MXene and MCCHS (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402178-fig-0002">2e</a>), respectively. The physical phase composition of the materials was characterized by X-ray diffraction (XRD) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402178-fig-0002">2f</a>). All SCMX samples inherited broad peaks in SC at 23.7° and 43.4°, which correspond to the (002) and (101) crystal planes of amorphous carbon, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402178-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402178-bib-0021_R_d216912174e792" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Energy Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> SCMXs also show Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>x</sub> (002) diffraction peaks at ≈5.82°, indicating that MXene interlayer spacing is 1.367&nbsp;nm, which facilitated the fast Na<sup>+</sup> transport.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402178-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402178-bib-0022_R_d216912174e806" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Science">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For SCMXs, the extra diffraction peaks at 25.32° and 27.27° are assigned to (101) planes of Ti<sub>0.72</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (JCPDS PDF#97-008-2084) and (110) planes of TiO<sub>2</sub> (JCPDS PDF#97-0016-9633). The appearance of titanium oxide is related to the partial oxidation of MXene during heat treatment, and the intensity of the diffraction peaks is enhanced with the increase of MXene content. In Raman spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402178-fig-0002">2g</a>), the peaks located at ≈160&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> of SCMXs are ascribed to the <i>E</i><sub>g</sub> vibration by symmetric stretching vibration of O−Ti−O originating from MXene.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402178-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402178-bib-0023_R_d216912174e827" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Chem. Eng. J.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, two broader D (1350&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>) and G peaks (1566–1600&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>) of carbon were observed in all samples,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402178-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402178-bib-0024_R_d216912174e836" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> implying the presence of a carbon component in SCMXs. In the Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FT-IR) spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6c</a>, Supporting Information), the peak of Ti−O−C bond at ≈853&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> appears for SCMX6, but not for SC and MXene, which indicates tight chemical bonding at the heterogeneous interface of MCCHS/MXene and stable structure of SCMX6.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402178-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402178-bib-0025_R_d216912174e847" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nat. Commun.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>To better understand the surface elemental states of SCMXs and the combined form of MCCHS and MXene, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) results were offered. All SCMX samples exhibited elemental peaks for C, O, S, and Ti, indicating the presence of MXene and S components (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6d</a>, Supporting Information). The high-resolution C 1<i>s</i> profiles of SC and SCMXs (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information) can be deconvoluted into six peaks C = C (284.6&nbsp;eV), C−S (285.8&nbsp;eV), C−O (286.9&nbsp;eV), C = O (288.3&nbsp;eV), O−C = O (289.5&nbsp;eV), and π−π* (≈291&nbsp;eV).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402178-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402178-bib-0026_R_d216912174e862" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Energy Stor. Mater. Adv. Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Compared to the SC (8.92%), SCMX6 has a higher C−S bond ratio (10.86%, Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information). The abundant C−S bond can change the spin density and charge distribution of the carbon matrix, contributing to the enhanced electrical conductivity and chemical reactivity of carbon.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402178-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402178-bib-0027_R_d216912174e871" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Energy Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the C−S bond can be reversibly reacted with Na<sup>+</sup> during the charging/discharging process, which greatly improves the sodium storage capacity and stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402178-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402178-bib-0028_R_d216912174e878" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Compared to the Ti(IV)−O 2<i>p</i> peaks (457.9 and 463.7&nbsp;eV) of MXene (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402178-fig-0002">2h</a>), the Ti(IV)−O 2<i>p</i> peaks of SCMX6 shifts toward higher binding energies (458.8 and 464.8&nbsp;eV). Meanwhile, a new peak at 456.5&nbsp;eV assigned as Ti−O−C bonds appeared in the SCMXs, confirming that MXene and MCCHS formed a stable chemical connection through covalent bonding, consistent with the FT-IR results.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402178-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402178-bib-0025_R_d216912174e891" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nat. Commun.">25</a>, <a href="#adfm202402178-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402178-bib-0029_R_d216912174e891" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Energy Mater.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Notably, the Ti−O−C bond was most abundant in the SCMX6 sample (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9c</a>, Supporting Information) because MXene formed most chemical connections with monolayer MCCHS. The high-resolution S 2<i>p</i> spectra of SC and SCMX6 in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402178-fig-0002">2i</a> can be divided into S 2<i>p</i><sub>3/2</sub> and S 2<i>p</i><sub>1/2</sub> orbitals of the C−S−C covalent bond (163.7 and 165.0&nbsp;eV) and S−O bond (167.2&nbsp;eV).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402178-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402178-bib-0030_R_d216912174e912" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Energy Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The S 2<i>p</i> spectra of the other materials are provided in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information). Additionally, a new peak at 161.6&nbsp;eV corresponding to the S−Ti bond emerges in SCMX6, which is consistent with the Ti 2<i>p</i> spectrum, and it can undergo a reversible conversion reaction with Na<sup>+</sup>, which is favorable for the enhancement of sodium storage performance. According to the nitrogen adsorption and desorption diagrams in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402178-fig-0002">2j</a>, both SC and SCMXs show isotherms of H3-type hysteresis loops in the medium and high-pressure regions, indicating the presence of abundant mesopores in the material. In addition, thanks to the homogeneous co-assembly of MCCHS and MXene, SCMX6 exhibited the largest specific surface area (617.6&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) and the largest pore volume (0.998&nbsp;cm<sup>3</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), avoiding the self-stacking of MCCHS or MXene.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402178-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402178-bib-0031_R_d216912174e939" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Energy Stor. Mater.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The pore size distribution of SCMX6 further indicates the presence of mesopores in the range of 4.26 to 4.66&nbsp;nm (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). The large number of mesopores facilitates the penetration of electrolytes and provides abundant channels for the rapid transport of sodium ions, which is conducive to electrochemical performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402178-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402178-bib-0032_R_d216912174e947" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Chem. Eng. J.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202402178-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202402178-sec-0050-title">2.3 Sodium Storage Behavior and Mechanism Characterization</h3>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">To understand the structural evolution of the electrode during cycling, we performed ex-situ high-resolution transmission electron microscope (HRTEM), in-/ex situ XRD, and ex situ XPS characterization of the SCMX6 (electrochemically optimized sample) electrode. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402178-fig-0003">3a,b</a> show the pristine and after cycles morphology of SCMX6, respectively, where many NPs were in situ generated on the carbon surface after cycling. Moreover, the NPs with a size of less than 10&nbsp;nm were uniformly distributed on the surface of the carbon matrix (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information). The inset in Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402178-fig-0003">3b</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12d</a> (Supporting Information) show interlayer spacing of 0.322 and 0.200&nbsp;nm, corresponding to the (111) and (220) crystal planes of Cu<sub>2</sub>S. During the cell reaction, the copper foil is slightly corroded and reacts with S in the active substance, gradually forming Cu<sub>2</sub>S NPs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402178-fig-0003">3c</a>), which has been reported in many literatures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402178-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402178-bib-0033_R_d216912174e983" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Energy Stor. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Cu<sub>2</sub>S NPs embedded in the bionic hierarchical architecture not only act as active sites, but also the hollow carbon can alleviate its volume expansion, fully utilizing the advantages of both. The intensity of the (002) peak of carbon exhibits periodic variations throughout the sodiation/desodiation process (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402178-fig-0003">3d</a>). During sodiation in the plateau region, a distinct differentiation in the peak intensity of the broad (002) peak was observed, indicating enhanced insertion of sodium ions into the carbon layers and nanopores.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402178-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402178-bib-0034_R_d216912174e994" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Mater.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To better observe the changes of S species in SCMX6 during cycling, ex-situ XRD was tested. The first cycle discharge up to 1.12&nbsp;V revealed the appearance of Na<sub>2</sub>S (JCPDS PDF#00-047-1698), Cu<sub>9</sub>S<sub>5</sub> (JCPDS PDF#97-005-3331), and NaCu<sub>5</sub>S<sub>3</sub> (JCPDS PDF#04-008-8929) peaks, as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402178-fig-0003">3e</a>, which can be attributed to the replacement reaction between Cu<sup>+</sup> from the Cu foil and S species within the carbon matrix.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202402178-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202402178-bib-0033_R_d216912174e1015" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Energy Stor. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These findings demonstrate the continuous generation of Cu<sub>x</sub>S and the subsequent incorporation of Na<sup>+</sup> into Cu<sub>x</sub>S to form an intercalation intermediate. When discharged to 0.01&nbsp;V, the S species was completely converted to Na<sub>2</sub>S. Upon charging to 1.85&nbsp;V, Na<sub>2</sub>S transforms to Cu<sub>2</sub>S (JCPDS PDF#00-053-0522) and Na<sub>3</sub>Cu<sub>4</sub>S<sub>4</sub> (JCPDS PDF#04-009-9325) intercalation intermediates. Eventually, S species were converted to Cu<sub>x</sub>S and a small number of intermediates when charging to 3.0&nbsp;V, which leads to an irreversible capacity of the first cycle. The charging/discharging curves and the corresponding ex situ XRD spectra of SCMX6 for the 3rd cycle are illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402178-fig-0003">3f</a>. The gradual transformation of Cu<sub>x</sub>S into Na<sub>y</sub>Cu<sub>x</sub>S and Na<sub>2</sub>S, as well as the reversible process during charging can be observed evidently. HRTEM was used to further characterize the structural evolution of the full sodiation/desodiation states of SCMX6. When fully discharged to 0.01&nbsp;V in the first cycle, the crystal lattices of Na<sub>2</sub>S corresponding to the (220) and (200) crystal planes (with an interlayer spacing of 0.228 and 0.321&nbsp;nm) were observed (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402178-fig-0003">3g</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13a</a>, Supporting Information), further demonstrating that S species in carbon matrix undergoes conversion reaction with Na<sup>+</sup>. TEM mapping images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402178-fig-0003">3h</a>) also demonstrate a uniform distribution of the elements S and Na, further illustrating the formation of Na<sub>2</sub>S. After fully charged to 3.0&nbsp;V, crystal lattices of 0.320–0.325&nbsp;nm corresponding to the (111) plane of Cu<sub>2</sub>S appeared (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402178-fig-0003">3i</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13b</a>, Supporting Information), indicating the in situ formation of Cu<sub>2</sub>S, which is consistent with the ex-situ XRD results. In addition, the TEM mapping image of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202402178-fig-0003">3j</a> demonstrates a uniform distribution of S and Cu, further proving the formation of Cu<sub>2</sub>S. The above ex-situ XRD and HRTEM results support the cyclic voltammetry (CV) tests, indicating that the S species in the carbon matrix undergoes a transition from C−S to Cu−S bonds and that Cu<sup>+</sup> dissolves from the Cu foil into the carbon matrix and reacts with the S species therein to form Cu<sub>2</sub>S NPs. The in-situ formed Cu<sub>2</sub>S NPs, which can act as active sites in SIBs, are beneficial for taking full use of our dual-biomimetic gradient hierarchical architecture. The mechanism of electrode reaction can be represented by the following equations:
